# 286\n
{Cambridge, Corpus Christi College 145, hand A, fols. 1r-210v: South English Legendary}\n
C14a1\n
429 195 N\n
{=NW Berks=}\n
{=Sample tagged: fols 63r-77r line 8; 82r line 11 - 92v line 18;\n
122r line 35 - 133r line 8 - Inventio Crucis, SS Quiriac, Brandan;\n
Barnabas, Theophilus, Alban, John the Baptist; James the Great,\n
Christopher, Martha, Oswald the King. Sections were chosen to\n
give some overlap with the 5 saints\' legends tagged from Laud Misc\n
108, hand A=}\n
{=Script - Anglicana=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The punctuation is very simple. Apart from paragraph markers - noted as\n
\'para\' inside braces - which appear at the beginning of lines at\n
irregular intervals, marking new sections or sometimes changes of\n
speaker, there is only the punctus. This occurs regularly to mark the\n
half-line and very irregularly - apparently more or less randomly - at\n
the end of the line. The punctus is usually placed somewhere between\n
the baseline and half-way up the height of an average ascender, but as\n
there seems to be no difference in its function according to its height,\n
I mark it as an ordinary punctus . not as a raised punctus ^. It is not\n
always clear from microfilm whether end of line punctus are intended or\n
not: some are quite faint and may be pen resting marks. I have recorded\n
these if they seem clear to me and if they are in the expected position\n
not too far from the final letter of the final word in the line. The\n
decorative \'tail\' on final <e> also sometimes ends with an extra press\n
of the pen which could be intended as a punctus, but I have only\n
recorded it as such if the point is separate from the tail and/or done\n
with a separate movement. Each line begins with a littera notabilior,\n
these are marked as capitals with * and are normally formed as\n
capitals, except those litterae whose figurae are not always\n
differentiated between majuscule and minuscule. New sections begin\n
with a large decorated capital - noted after the word - followed by a\n
smaller capital letter. On the microfilm it looks as if the letter\n
immediately following the midline punctus is rubricated. I do not mark\n
these with * as they are not majuscule in shape, but the rubrication\n
is a useful guide to where missing or misplaced punctus should be\n
situated - I have noted any exceptions to the general rule=}\n
{~f63r~}\n
$/TOd_*y*E\n

$/TOd_yE\n
$sweet/ajOd_SWETE\n
$tree/nOd_TRE[O]\n
{=O in words with final EO is habitually erased, I assume by a corrector\n
rather than the main scribe. This assumption is based on the fact that\n
other corrections are made in a hand and ink different from the main\n
scribe. I use [] to indicate where it happens as in d\'Evelyn and\n
Mill\'s EETS edition (d\'E&M). Midword the corrector has not usually\n
tried to alter EO. I take it therefore that EO was the spelling\n
intended by the original scribe; the brackets indicate merely that\n
the letter is not now wholly visible. This contrasts with the\n
correction of EO to E in the Ormulum where Orm was apparently\n
responsible both for the original spelling and for the removal\n
of the O. In the Ormulum therefore, I transcribe E<O< in such cases\n
rather than E[O]=}\n
{.}\n

company - hence zE plural. But then he seems to change to yOU,\n
presumably addressing Brandan alone for 2 lines before changing\n
back to addressing everyone. Otherwise we would have to assume\n
a change from \'polite\' zE to \'familiar\' yOU and back again. The\n
text in Laud 108 makes the switch clear, but the added line that\n
does so spoils the rhyme scheme, so was presumably added to resolve\n
the ambiguity=}\n
